Title: Default Contagion, Matrix Approximation, and Control in Sparse Financial Networks

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.24833v1

Published Time: Tue, 26 May 2026 01:15:23 GMT

Number of Pages: 119

Markdown Content:
# Default Contagion, Matrix Approximation, and Control in Sparse Financial Networks 

Aoxin Zhang ‚ąó Yingzhe Wang ‚Ä†

May 26, 2026 

Abstract 

We study systemic default contagion in sparse financial networks and develop a framework for deciding when aggregate exposure matrices are reliable and when node-level network information changes tail risk and control design. The first contribution is a multi-population McKean‚ÄďVlasov foundation for distance-to-default dynamics with common noise, bounded state-dependent killing, loss feedback, sparse weighted exposures, and regulatory intervention, including quantitative convergence, propagation of chaos, stability in contagion matrices, controlled well-posedness, a two-population HJB characterization, and a steep-killing bridge to absorbing-boundary contagion. The second contribution is a set of computable matrix-approximation diagnostics: finite-grid bounds driven by row-exposure dispersion and square-edge spread, constructive tail-loss gaps for networks sharing the same aggregate matrix, and a spectral-radius criterion for local cascade onset. The third contribution is an information-value theory for control, showing that node-level graph pressure has strictly positive value when within-type pressure variation interacts with nonsaturated marginal killing reduction. Matched sparse-graph and matrix experiments, common-noise tests, HJB feedback diagnostics, fixed-budget control comparisons, and EBA/Pillar 3-calibrated synthetic networks validate the framework. The main conclusion is that finite-type matrices are effective in regular-mixing regimes, whereas concentrated sparse exposures generate tail-risk and intervention effects that require local-pressure diagnostics and network-aware control. 

Keywords. systemic risk, financial networks, McKean‚ÄďVlasov equations, default contagion, sparse graphs, common noise, optimal control, matrix approximation 

MSC 2020. 91G40, 91G80, 60H30, 60K35, 93E20, 49L20, 05C82 

# 1 Introduction 

Systemic default risk is generated by an interaction between balance-sheet fragility and network structure. The clearing-network model of Eisenberg and Noe [ 1 ] provides the standard limited-liability benchmark, while Allen and Gale [ 2], Gai and Kapadia [ 3 ], Acemoglu, Ozdaglar, and Tahbaz-Salehi [ 4 ], Elliott, Golub, and Jackson [ 5], and Glasserman and Young [ 6 ] show that diversification, connectivity, and shock size have non-monotone implications for cascade risk. A parallel large-population literature models default contagion through interacting particles and McKean‚ÄďVlasov equations. Hambly, Ledger, and S√łjmark [ 7 ], Hambly and S√łjmark [ 8 ], and Hambly 

> ‚ąó

> arXiv:2605.24833v1 [math.OC] 24 May 2026

and Jettkant [ 9] show how distance-to-default dynamics, loss feedback, state-dependent killing, and control can be represented through low-dimensional distributional dynamics. The practical tension is that regulatory stress tests often need aggregate and interpretable objects, while real interbank networks remain sparse, heterogeneous, and locally concentrated. A finite-type contagion matrix is attractive because it summarizes cross-type exposures, supports spectral and row-exposure diagnostics, and leads to tractable control rules. Sparse financial networks, however, can transmit losses through hub nodes, core‚Äďperiphery concentration, and local high-pressure neighborhoods that disappear under type averaging. This paper studies the boundary between these two representations by constructing a sparse graph contagion system and an aggregate matrix approximation from the same graph. The two systems share node types, initial states, kill intensities, loss feedback, and aggregate exposure sources, so their difference isolates the information loss caused by aggregating local network structure. This boundary also matters for systemic-risk measurement and policy design. Market-based measures such as systemic expected shortfall and CoVaR quantify institution-level contributions to aggregate distress [ 10 , 11 ]; clearing and network-contagion models explain how balance-sheet links propagate distress once a shock has entered the system. Our focus is the interface between these two viewpoints: we ask when a regulator can work with a low-dimensional exposure matrix, and when tail-risk measurement requires additional local graph information. This framing leads to a concrete information question. If two networks have the same type-level matrix but different within-block concentration, then any difference in tail loss, cascade probability, or intervention cost is attributable to information discarded by the matrix projection. The paper therefore combines a regularized McKean‚ÄďVlasov limit with finite-network diagnostics. We show that the matrix‚Äďnetwork boundary is theoretically characterizable through computable graph diagnostics and practically consequential for regulatory control design. The regularization through bounded state-dependent killing keeps the default mechanism close to absorbing-boundary contagion while leaving the controlled system compatible with dynamic programming. The sparse-network layer is finite and constructive: it compares matched graph and matrix systems under identical shocks and budgets, so the empirical design tests the same approximation boundary described by the theory. 

Main contributions The first contribution is a multi-population McKean‚ÄďVlasov foundation for regularized default contagion with common noise, loss feedback, and control. We prove the large-system limit, a conditional O(N ‚ąí1/2 

> ‚ąó

) first-order convergence rate under explicit quadratic empirical-process and metric-stability inputs, same-type propagation of chaos, controlled well-posedness, and Lipschitz stability with respect to the contagion matrix. This foundation also supports two control-facing extensions: a two-population HJB characterization in a standard lifted comparison framework, and a steep-killing bridge to absorbing-boundary contagion under nonsticky-boundary, uniform-map-continuity, and local fixed-point-stability conditions. The result is a rigorous bounded-killing theory with a transparent interface to the absorbing-boundary and viscosity tools used in systemic-risk control. The second contribution is a structural characterization of the matrix-approximation boundary. We derive a finite-grid absolute graph-to-matrix expected-loss bound controlled by two computable structural quantities: within-block row-exposure dispersion and square-edge exposure spread. A share-controlled corollary then translates the square-edge term into a maximum-edge-share diagnostic. We also prove a heterogeneous-network tail-gap construction, embed it in any larger finite type system, and connect local cascade onset to a spectral-radius threshold under an explicit low-loss second-order approximation. The numerical design then uses stochastic block, core‚Äďperiphery, 2heavy-tailed, Barab√°si‚ÄďAlbert, and Watts‚ÄďStrogatz networks to show how the same aggregate matrix can underestimate sparse-graph tail loss when local exposure concentration is high. The third contribution is a control and information-value analysis. We give an explicit variational sufficient condition under which node-level graph-pressure information has strictly positive control value near the matrix-approximation boundary, and we verify primitive pressure-monotonicity conditions for exponential killing in a nonsaturated local window. The rule-based experiments show where local pressure information gives low-cost cascade reduction, while the finite-dimensional 

K = 2 HJB loop turns the measure-valued Hamiltonian characterization into a computable feedback benchmark. The EBA Transparency Exercise and Pillar 3/EDAP calibration layer further tests whether the mechanism remains visible under external regulatory data constraints. The rest of the paper is organized as follows. Section 2 positions the paper in the financial-network, McKean‚ÄďVlasov, and graph-limit literatures. Section 3 defines the finite-type and sparse-graph contagion systems. Sections 4‚Äď7 state the main theoretical results. Sections 8‚Äď14 present the numerical and calibration evidence, and Section 15 discusses implications and limitations. 

# 2 Related Literature 

2.1 Financial networks and default cascades 

We now expand on the financial-network, interacting-particle, and graph-limit literatures introduced above, with emphasis on the technical distinction between aggregate exposure matrices and local sparse-network information. Network models of systemic risk begin from the observation that a bank‚Äôs default losses depend on the payment, funding, liquidity, and asset-sale behavior of other institutions. The clearing-vector framework of Eisenberg and Noe [ 1] and the default-cost extension of Rogers and Veraart [ 12 ] provide benchmark models for mutual-liability networks. Liquidity and fire-sale channels are developed by Cifuentes, Ferrucci, and Shin [ 13 ], Gai, Haldane, and Kapadia [ 14 ], Caballero and Simsek [ 15 ], and May and Arinaminpathy [ 16 ]. Empirical and stylized interbank-network studies by Boss et al. [ 17 ], Nier et al. [ 18 ], Cont, Moussa, and Santos [ 19 ], Battiston et al. [ 20 ], Glasserman and Young [ 21 ], Caccioli et al. [ 22 ], Capponi, Chen, and Yao [ 23 ], and Gandy and Veraart [ 24 ] show that degree distributions, exposure concentration, centrality, and balance-sheet fragility jointly determine cascade paths. Empirical high-dimensional bank-network estimation, such as Demirer, Diebold, Liu, and Yilmaz [25], further reinforces the role of cross-bank connectedness in crisis periods. The robust-yet-fragile mechanism is especially important for this paper. Allen and Gale [ 2 ], Gai and Kapadia [ 3 ], Acemoglu, Ozdaglar, and Tahbaz-Salehi [ 4 ], Elliott, Golub, and Jackson [ 5 ], Amini, Cont, and Minca [ 26 ], Gleeson [ 27 ], Hurd, Gleeson, and Melnik [ 28 ], and Glasserman and Young [ 6]all point to a common lesson: aggregate connectivity can stabilize small shocks while amplifying losses once local cascades start. Our sparse-graph experiments follow this line, but the object of comparison is deliberately narrower. We compare a sparse graph system with the finite-type matrix obtained from the same graph, so the measured gap is the price of type aggregation rather than a difference across unrelated network specifications. 

2.2 Mean-field contagion, McKean‚ÄďVlasov models, and control 

Mean-field contagion models describe the dynamic state of a large financial system through empirical measures and loss processes; the broader systemic-risk modeling background is surveyed in Fouque and Langsam [ 29 ]. Diffusion-based mechanisms for systemic risk and interbank stability are analyzed in Fouque and Ichiba [ 30 ] and Garnier, Papanicolaou, and Yang [ 31 ]. Hambly, Ledger, and S√łjmark [ 7]3study McKean‚ÄďVlasov equations with positive feedback and blow-up, Hambly and S√łjmark [ 8]derive an SPDE model for systemic risk with endogenous contagion, and Ledger and S√łjmark [ 32 ]analyze uniqueness in the weak-feedback regime. Delarue et al. [ 33 ] provide a related particle-approximation theory for singular mean-field self-excitation, while Sznitman [ 34 ] and Budhiraja, Dupuis, and Fischer [ 35 ] give standard propagation-of-chaos and weak-convergence reference points for interacting-particle limits. Feinstein and S√łjmark [ 36 , 37 ] connect dynamic interbank default contagion with heterogeneous McKean‚ÄďVlasov limits, while Hambly and Jettkant [ 9 ] study state-dependent killing and central control. We use state-dependent killing as a regularized default mechanism because it keeps default probabilities state sensitive and gives a stable interface for finite-dimensional control diagnostics. The control side draws on McKean‚ÄďVlasov control, infinite-dimensional HJB theory, numerical methods for McKean‚ÄďVlasov equations, and network mean-field methods. Huang, Malham√©, and Caines [ 38 ], Carmona, Fouque, and Sun [ 39 ], and Carmona and Delarue [ 40 ] provide the probabilistic background for mean-field control, mean-field games, and systemic-risk control; Fabbri, Gozzi, and Swiech [ 41 ] give dynamic programming and HJB theory in infinite dimensions; Crandall, Ishii, and Lions [ 42 ] provide the classical viscosity comparison framework; Cosso et al. [ 43 ] provide a comparison principle for Bellman equations on Wasserstein space, while Gangbo, M√©sz√°ros, Mou, and Zhang [ 44 ] give a complementary master-equation reference point under displacement monotonicity; and Chassagneux, Crisan, and Delarue [ 45 ] give numerical methods for McKean‚ÄďVlasov FBSDEs. Deep-BSDE methods such as Han, Jentzen, and E [ 46 ] provide an alternative high-dimensional PDE benchmark, but our finite-dimensional HJB loop is used as a transparent diagnostic rather than a black-box solver. Graph-limit and sparse-network perspectives enter through Lov√°sz [ 47 ], Benjamini and Schramm [ 48 ], Borgs et al. [ 49 ], Delattre, Giacomin, and Lu√ßon [ 50 ], Oliveira and Reis [ 51 ], Oliveira, Reis, and Stolerman [ 52 ], Parise and Ozdaglar [ 53 ], Caines and Huang [ 54 ], Carmona, Cooney, Graves, and Lauri√®re [ 55 ], Aurell, Carmona, DayanńĪklńĪ, and Lauri√®re [ 56 ], Bayraktar, Chakraborty, and Wu [ 57 ], Lacker and Soret [ 58 ], and Bendel [ 59 ]. These works motivate our central question: when is a finite-type matrix a valid low-dimensional projection of sparse financial contagion, and when does node-level pressure information become necessary for tail-risk control?

The tail gap is the main diagnostic, because local network structure can have a limited effect on mean loss while substantially changing tail paths. The matrix-approximation theorem below uses two graph-level quantities that are computed directly from the adjacency matrix. The first is within-block row-exposure dispersion: nodes in the same target type should receive similar total exposure from each source type if the type matrix is to represent them accurately. The second is square-edge exposure spread: realized default-set fluctuations within a source type are small when exposure is distributed over many source nodes and large when a few edges dominate. In stochastic block networks these quantities are controlled by Bernoulli sampling and edge-weight moments. In core‚Äďperiphery and heavy-tailed networks they become larger exactly when local concentration creates information that is lost under type aggregation.

The numerical part uses two complementary routes. The first route compares three interpretable control rules: the no-control rule serves as the benchmark; type-uniform control applies the same type-level control intensity to all nodes and represents a simple high-coverage regulatory intervention; graph-pressure threshold control triggers intervention according to node-level local pressure P graph  

> i,t

or its smoothed version, and can therefore act on high-pressure nodes before local shocks are reflected in type-average losses. The second route solves a finite-dimensional projection HJB in the case K = 2 , constructs feedback control consistent with the first-order condition in Theorem 6, and compares it with rule-based controls in the same forward particle system. The resulting control conclusions are organized as risk‚Äďcost frontiers and comparisons of information structures. 64 Mean-field limit and quantitative convergence 

This section gives the mean-field foundation of the regularized default contagion model in the main text. We keep only theorem statements, proof sketches, and the explanations needed for the numerical design. Full technical proofs are given in Appendix SM2. 

Convention for the theoretical results The economic specification in (5) and in the kill-intensity equations above uses an exponential state-dependent kill intensity. All rigorous results in Sections 4‚Äď7 are stated for the corresponding bounded regularized killing specification: throughout these results we assume that there exists őõ < ‚ąě such that

as a direct consequence of the bounded-Lipschitz killing estimates in the appendix comparison-principle proof. In the finite-dimensional HJB computation, the state grid is compact and the cap can be chosen above the maximum exponential intensity attained on the grid. Proposition 4 and the appendix control proofs give the corresponding steep-killing bridge to absorbing-boundary contagion under nonsticky boundary crossing, absence of conditional atoms in the limiting default time, uniform steep-limit continuity of the driven default map, and local stability of the absorbing-boundary McKean‚ÄďVlasov fixed point. 

Theorem 1 (multi-population McKean‚ÄďVlasov limit) Fix T > 0. Consider a system with 

K types of financial institutions, where type k has Nk particles and N‚ąó := min k Nk ‚Üí ‚ąě . Assume that initial states are independent and identically distributed within each type and have uniformly finite first moments, which are sufficient for the qualitative limit; the second-moment bound used for the quantitative rate is imposed separately in Theorem 2. Assume also that the drift bk is Lipschitz in the state and measure variables, the kill intensity őĽk is bounded and Lipschitz, and the contagion matrix őď is bounded. The type-level control at = ( a1

More precisely, őľk,N t converges weakly to őľkt , and Lk,N t converges uniformly in probability on [0 , T ].The proof uses synchronous coupling: the finite particles and the limiting particles share initial states, idiosyncratic noises, common noise, and Poisson thinning random measures. The common-noise adapted control convention preserves conditional independence inside each type once the 7common shock history is fixed. The drift error, killing error, and loss-feedback error are controlled by Lipschitz conditions, and the estimate closes by Gronwall‚Äôs inequality. This result gives the finite-type matrix experiments a rigorous large-system limit foundation. The uniqueness of the limiting controlled system is established independently in Proposition 3, which proves well-posedness under the same bounded-killing and Lipschitz regularity. 

Theorem 2 (quantitative convergence rate under explicit empirical-process inputs) 

Under the conditions of Theorem 1, assume also that the system has a uniform second-moment bound, that the empirical measures of the conditionally independent limiting particles satisfy the standard bounded-Lipschitz quadratic fluctuation estimate, and that the synchronous Poisson-thinning coupling satisfies the corresponding metric-level quadratic bounded-Lipschitz stability estimate. This last estimate means that the bounded-Lipschitz distance between the finite surviving empirical measure and the paired limiting auxiliary empirical measure is controlled in mean square by the state, measure, and loss coupling errors, up to the usual N ‚ąí1

This rate follows by tracking constants in the synchronous coupling argument for Theorem 1 and by estimating the loss process through its Doob‚ÄďMeyer martingale decomposition. Common noise changes only the conditional distribution and the constants; it does not change the empirical fluctuation order driven by the minimum type sample size. We state the quadratic bounded-Lipschitz empirical fluctuation input and the synchronous-thinning metric stability input explicitly because the killed-particle setting requires metric-level control of the bounded-Lipschitz supremum. Under the localized finite-entropy/Donsker condition recorded in Appendix SM2, these inputs hold with constants independent of the type sample size, and the displayed estimate is an unconditional consequence after integrating over the common noise. The empirical one-dimensional W 22 diagnostics reported below are numerical stability diagnostics consistent with the same first-order N ‚ąí1/2

Remark (primitive sufficient condition for Theorem 2) The abstract inputs in Theorem 2 are verified under the localized finite-entropy condition for the killed-path bounded-Lipschitz class and the compact-state localization recorded in Lemma SM2.3 and Remark SM2.4 of Appendix SM2. In that primitive regime, the estimate in (16) follows from the bounded-killing, Lipschitz, and finite-entropy assumptions rather than from an additional rate postulate.

# 5 Matrix approximation error, tail gap, and spectral radius 

This section formulates testable theoretical propositions for the relation between sparse graphs and finite-type matrices. Full proofs are given in Appendix SM3. 

Theorem 3 (finite-grid absolute structural bound for the expected-loss gap of matrix approximation) Let Őā őď be the type-aggregated matrix of a sparse graph A, and run the graph system and the finite-type matrix system on the same fixed discrete grid ‚ąÜ = {0 = t0 < ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ <tM = T }. The two systems are synchronously coupled: they use the same initial states, Brownian increments, common-noise increments, and killing randomization variables. At each grid step, drift and diffusion are applied first, new defaults are sampled from the bounded state-dependent killing probability, and contagion shocks are then applied as one-step state jumps through the graph pressure ‚ąĎ

Scope of the structural bound When the within-type sampling regularity holds, Theorem 3 gives a certified finite-grid upper bound on the expected-loss discrepancy. In heavy-tailed, core‚Äďperiphery, and other locally concentrated networks, DR(A) and DS (A) remain computable diagnostics for the row-dispersion and square-edge channels through which type aggregation loses information. The theorem identifies these structural mechanisms, and the simulations measure their realized size under strong local concentration. The same discrete proof is stable under Euler-type scalings in which one-step default probabilities are of order ‚ąÜt and the per-default shock scale őĪ(‚ąÜ tm) satisfies boundedness of ‚ąĎ

) ‚Č• őī. (18) Theorem 4 shows that local exposure concentration can create a strictly positive tail-loss gap even when the aggregate matrices are identical. The aggregate matrix therefore identifies average exposure but leaves the local shock distribution and tail cascade probability underdetermined once concentrated exposures are allowed. This result is the theoretical prototype for the sparse graph experiments in Sections 10‚Äď11. The same construction embeds in any finite type system with at least two active types under the identical inactive-block or no-atom shifted-quantile conditions recorded in Appendix SM2ary 10 embedding result. Choosing one source type and one target type, setting all other type blocks identical across the two graph sequences, and keeping their terminal losses deterministic or common across the two systems preserves the same positive quantile separation after a harmless change in type shares. Thus the two-type construction is the minimal version of a general finite-type information-loss mechanism. The separation condition has an explicit feasible parameter region. For example, take the core-type share ŌÄ1 = 0 .05 and the periphery-type share ŌÄ2 = 0 .95 . Let the additional shock-probability difference from the core hub to periphery nodes be p+ ‚ąí p‚ąí = 0 .05 , and take the initial perturbation cost of the first type to be ŌĀ = 0 .08 . Then 

ŌÄ2(p+ ‚ąí p‚ąí) ‚ąí ŌÄ1ŌĀ = 0 .95 √ó 0.05 ‚ąí 0.05 √ó 0.08 = 0 .0435 > 0.

Under the exponential kill intensity őĽ0(x) = őĽbase exp {ő≥(xb ‚ąí x)}, the stated p+ ‚ąí p‚ąí can be generated by pushing the post-hub-shock state near the boundary while the uniform shock with the same aggregate exposure remains outside the boundary, and by increasing ő≥ or the near-critical exposure multiplier. The construction is taken on a discrete-time sampling convention in which first-stage defaults are sampled before the contagion shock of that step is fed back into later kill probabilities; choosing the first window sufficiently short keeps earlier feedback effects of order ‚ąÜt

and preserves the separation. The lower bound in Theorem 4 is therefore realized in an explicit transition region with feasible parameters.

Remark on the finite-sample compatibility in Theorem 5 The logarithmic-window condition is the finite-sample requirement that makes the probability statement meaningful. In the formulation recorded in the appendix proof, the deterministic residual and martingale fluctuation scale must be 11 small relative to the Perron‚ÄďFrobenius growth generated by the initial seed before the trajectory reaches the local threshold. A representative compatible regime has residual order O(N ‚ąí1) and seed size ‚ą•Y N 

> 0

‚ą•1 = N ‚ąíőĪ with 0 < őĪ < 1, together with the corresponding martingale domination on the same window. This is a local theorem, not a global cascade theorem. The assumptions above are the main-text version of the low-loss expansion, finite-sample fluctuation control, nondegenerate Perron‚Äď Frobenius seed, and logarithmic-window domination conditions proved and interpreted in the appendix approximation proofs. The result explains why we use ŌĀ(őď) as a diagnostic for contagion amplification in the low-loss linearized region. The spectral radius organizes local amplification, while the graph diagnostics in Theorem 3 describe the within-type information that remains invisible after type aggregation. The numerical sparse-graph experiments combine these two diagnostics.

# 6 Controlled contagion systems and optimal control characteriza-tion 

This section gives the well-posedness of the controlled model, the optimal control characterization for two populations, and the relation between the regularized killing mechanism and the hard absorbing boundary. Proposition 4 is a singular-limit statement and is separate from the bounded-killing framework used in the main limit and control theorems. Full proofs are given in Appendix SM4. 

Proposition 3 (well-posedness of the controlled multi-population contagion system) 

Assume that the drift is Lipschitz in the state and measure variables with linear growth, the kill intensity is bounded and Lipschitz in the state variable, the control sets are compact, the contagion matrix is bounded, the diffusion coefficients are bounded, and the initial states have the required first or second moments. Then, for any common-noise adapted admissible type-level control, the controlled multi-population McKean‚ÄďVlasov contagion system has a unique solution. The loss process is a c√†dl√†g nondecreasing process, and the solution is stable with respect to the initial distribution and the control flow. This proposition also supplies the uniqueness input invoked in Theorem 1. The proof constructs a map from measure flows and loss flows to themselves. The map is closed by a contraction argument on short time intervals, and a concatenation argument then gives a global solution on [0 , T ]. This proposition ensures that the optimization object in (15) is well defined. 

Theorem 6 (existence of two-population optimal control and conditional common-noise HJB characterization) Consider the controlled contagion system with K = 2 . The type-level 12 control takes values in the compact set [0 , ¬Įa]2, the kill intensity is bounded and Lipschitz on the compact state-control domain, the controlled martingale problem is well posed, the idiosyncratic alive-state diffusion coefficients satisfy ŌÉk > 0, the cost functions ck are continuous and convex on compact control sets, and the terminal loss penalty ő¶ : [0 , 1] 2 ‚Üí R is bounded uniformly continuous; in the numerical quadratic-loss specifications it is bounded automatically because (L1 

> T

, L 2 

> T

) ‚ąą [0 , 1] 2.Then, in the presence of common noise, the central control problem admits an optimal relaxed control. The compact relaxed-control formulation gives the HJB value. The killed-HJB comparison principle is proved in the appendix comparison-principle proof on the decomposed state space of alive sub-probability measures and cemetery masses, which is identified with M‚Č§1,2(R)2 √ó [0 , 1] 2 subject to |őĹk| + Lk = 1 . The alive-measure variables are handled by the Wasserstein smooth-gauge/Ishii template, the cemetery masses are handled by finite-dimensional doubled-variable penalties, and the killing jump becomes a bounded Lipschitz first-order perturbation from alive mass to cemetery mass. Under the standard chattering and measurable-selection conditions recorded in the appendix control proofs, the relaxed value agrees with the strict-control value whenever the Hamiltonian minimizer admits a progressively measurable strict selector. With this convention, the value function is the unique bounded uniformly continuous viscosity solution of the corresponding decomposed measure-valued HJB equation, equivalently of the lifted killed-state HJB. The preceding viscosity characterization does not require classical differentiability of the value function. The first-order condition below is a smooth-verification diagnostic rather than an additional viscosity-uniqueness claim. When, in addition, the value function admits a classical verification on a smooth region of the lifted measure space, so that the Lions derivative with respect to őľk and the corresponding cemetery-jump marginal value are well defined, the Hamiltonian minimizer can be characterized by a first-order condition. In the finite-dimensional projected HJB used numerically, this smoothness requirement can be checked locally by standard parabolic PDE regularity on compact grids when the projected diffusion in the alive coordinates is nondegenerate and the coefficients are bounded Lipschitz; the paper uses the resulting formula only as a Hamiltonian diagnostic. Under the stated comparison, relaxed-control, and selector inputs, this theorem places the rule-based controls used in this paper within a stochastic control framework. In the numerical section, we solve the finite-dimensional projection HJB for K = 2 and compare the resulting feedback control with rule-based controls. This projection is the computational counterpart of the measure-valued characterization. The comparison principle is proved for the bounded regularized killing specification with K = 2 ; extension to general K requires a K-fold product Wasserstein smooth-gauge construction and a complete doubled-variable maximum argument for all cross-type terms. The numerical HJB benchmark is correspondingly kept at K = 2 , a natural core‚Äďperiphery 13 partition in which systemically important institutions and other banks form the two interacting populations. 

Proposition 4 (steep killing and absorbing-boundary default) The regularized killing mechanism used in the paper has a steep-intensity limit that selects an absorbing-boundary model. The appendix control proofs give a primitive verification of the bridge under a localized one-dimensional boundary-regularity package: compact localization, bounded Lipschitz frozen coefficients, nondegenerate idiosyncratic diffusion near the boundary, uniform killed-diffusion boundary estimates, and an absorbing no-atom modulus. The appendix proof verifies localized uniqueness of the absorbing fixed point by a Volterra short-time contraction and concatenation argument, derives eventual entrance into a stability neighborhood, proves local stability of the absorbing fixed point, and obtains uniform convergence of the driven steep-killing maps on compact localized classes. The limiting object depends on the baseline intensity above the boundary, which makes the proposition a precise bridge between regularized killing and the absorbing-boundary convention used in hard-default models. If 

őĽ(n)0 (x) = őĽbase exp( n(xb ‚ąí x)+),

then the selected limit is absorbing-boundary default together with the baseline Cox killing that remains active while x ‚Č• xb. If the intended limiting object is a pure hard absorbing boundary with no baseline Cox killing above the boundary, one can use 

> ňú

őĽ(n)0 (x) = őĽbase {exp( n(xb ‚ąí x)+) ‚ąí 1},

or let the above-boundary baseline intensity vanish with n. The numerical experiments use the bounded regularized state-dependent killing mechanism directly; this singular-limit proposition identifies the absorbing-boundary model selected by a separate steep-killing limit. 

# 7 Information value of local pressure information 

Theorem 7 (variational sufficient condition for positive value of local pressure infor-mation) Let Ftype be the filtration generated by type-average losses and type-average pressures, and let Fgraph be the filtration that further includes node-level local pressure P graph

with P(E) > 0 on which the node-level graph pressures of surviving type-k nodes have nondegenerate within-type variance. The comparison is made on a strong construction in which the baseline type-level optimizer and the graph-pressure reallocated control share the same Brownian motions and killing randomization variables; the reallocated control agrees with the baseline control up to ŌĄ , so E and the pressure vector used for reallocation are observed under both controls at the intervention time. Assume also that the conditional terminal-risk functional is twice Gateaux differentiable along budget-preserving node-level reallocations in the centered pressure direction Z. Finally, assume 14 a strict marginal-benefit condition: there exists mk > 0 such that reallocating an infinitesimal unit of control toward nodes with larger Zi lowers the conditional terminal cost by at least mkSk up to the stated type-share and active-window constants, and the second-order remainder is bounded by CőĶ 2Sk. V type ‚ąí V graph ‚Č• őīinfo . (19) The theorem is a sufficient condition for positive information value. The proof constructs a graph-measurable reallocation that shifts intervention from low-pressure nodes to high-pressure nodes while preserving the first-order control budget. The strict marginal-benefit condition turns this reallocation into a strict reduction in terminal loss risk. The appendix control proofs verify primitive pressure monotonicity for exponential killing in a nonsaturated local window, showing how the marginal-benefit constant is generated by the model. They also record a sufficient smoothness regime for the twice Gateaux differentiability input: smooth bounded drift and killing coefficients, bounded smooth terminal loss penalty, compact controls away from saturation corners, and a local window on which conditional killing compensators have uniformly bounded derivatives. In saturated regions, the forward experiments complement the variational lower bound by reporting realized risk‚Äďcost comparisons. The graph-pressure threshold control experiments in Section 11 are the numerical counterpart of this variational mechanism. 

Corollary 1 (primitive pressure-monotonicity window for Theorem 7) Suppose the kill intensity has the exponential form őĽk(x, a ) = őĽ0,k (x) exp {‚ąí ő∑ka}, the relevant stopping-time event lies in a nonsaturated window in which controls are interior and the post-pressure state remains inside a compact neighborhood of the boundary, and the second-order feedback terms along centered pressure reallocations are bounded. Then the marginal-benefit condition in Theorem 7 holds with a positive constant determined by the killing sensitivity ő∑k, the local baseline intensity, the drift damping, and the active-window survival mass. Consequently, whenever the within-type local-pressure variance is nondegenerate on such a window, node-level graph-pressure information has strictly positive value relative to type-average information. The appendix proof gives the corresponding pressure-monotonicity calculation and the explicit second-order remainder bound. 

# 8 Numerical design 

The finite-type experiments use four classes of institutions: small banks, regional banks, major banks, and systemically important banks. The moderate-stress specification sets the baseline kill-intensity multiplier to 2.5 and shifts the initial state downward by ‚ąí0.25 . The matrix scalings are 45 , 48 , 50 ,the particle number is 10000 , the number of time steps is 500 , and the number of replications is 

200 . This specification is used to display near-critical loss growth and the risk‚Äďcost frontier in the matrix system. The convergence validation experiment changes the particle number under the same moderate-stress structure and estimates the reference loss and reference empirical distribution using a large-sample system. The sparse graph experiments examine three network classes. The stochastic block network serves as the regular-mixing benchmark, with edge probabilities and edge weights mainly determined by type pairs. The core‚Äďperiphery network increases the connection strength and spillover exposure of the core type, and is used to represent locally concentrated risk around systemically important nodes. The heavy-tailed network introduces source-node fitness and hub exposure multipliers, and is used to represent cascade risk driven by highly connected nodes. Each graph is aggregated into a 15 matrix approximation through (11) , and the graph system and matrix system are compared under the same default mechanism. Sparse graph exposure intensity is amplified by a common multiplier x, with scan range 

x = 1 , . . . , 30 . The representative control experiments choose three points: the heavy-tailed network at x = 12 , the core‚Äďperiphery network at x = 15 , and the stochastic block network at x = 26 . At these points, the true graph systems already exhibit significant cascades, while the corresponding aggregate matrix approximations have not entered the cascade region at the same time. The synthetic numerical experiments use unified inputs and outputs. Inputs include type shares 

ŌÄk, the initial safety-margin distribution, drift and volatility parameters, kill-intensity parameters, the contagion matrix or sparse adjacency matrix, common-noise intensity, control rules and their budgets or thresholds, the time grid, the particle number, and the number of Monte Carlo replications. The finite-type matrix experiments specify őď directly. The sparse graph experiments first generate 

A and then obtain the paired matrix Őā őď from (11) . Outputs are unified as terminal mean loss, 95% 

tail loss, cascade probability, control cost, risk reduction per unit cost, and the mean and tail gaps between the graph system and the matrix system. This organization allows different network families, control rules, and parameter scans to be compared under the same set of risk‚Äďcost metrics, rather than treating each experiment as an independent simulation case. To test the consistency between the theoretical results and numerical mechanisms, we include several closed-loop experiments. First, we vary the particle number N and estimate the reference loss using a large-sample system, and we use mean-square error to validate the N ‚ąí1/2 

> ‚ąó

particle scale in the conditional quantitative rate of Theorem 2. Since the figure reports MSE, the corresponding reference slope is ‚ąí1, and we report the log‚Äďlog regression slope, standard error, R2, and significance level. Second, we scan the common-noise intensity ŌÉ0 to study how common shocks amplify terminal loss, tail gaps, and local control value. Third, we compute the row-dispersion component Rk(A)

and the square-edge-spread component Sk(A), and compare the resulting matrix-approximation diagnostic with realized graph‚Äďmatrix gaps. Fourth, we rescale the three network classes to the same aggregate spectral radius to isolate the contribution of network heterogeneity itself to the matrix approximation gap. Fifth, we compare type-uniform control and graph-pressure threshold control again under a fixed total budget, so that the risk‚Äďcost comparison does not depend on cost differences naturally generated by different strategies. Sixth, we densely sample the transition region 

x = 8 , . . . , 16 and use high-replication Monte Carlo estimation to report cascade probabilities, tail gaps, and confidence intervals. Seventh, we add time-varying networks, extra degree-distribution networks, and finite-type matrix experiments with K = 3 , 4 to test the robustness of the conclusions to network dynamics, degree distributions, and the dimension of the population partition. The two-population optimal control experiment uses a core‚Äďperiphery structure. The core type has share 5% , and the periphery type has share 95% . The control computation solves a finite-dimensional projection HJB on (x1, x 2, L 1, L 2), and compares HJB feedback, type-uniform control, graph-pressure threshold control, and no control in the same forward particle system. The HJB calibration selects a nondegenerate near-critical region in which the uncontrolled system exhibits clear cascades and the optimal feedback has nonzero intervention intensity at comparable cost. The numerical discretization uses a semi-implicit diffusion step for the second-order terms in the x direction, retains the common-noise cross term, and uses a one-way semi-Lagrangian loss jump with monotone piecewise-linear interpolation in the loss coordinates to handle the advance of the killing term in the L coordinate. We then solve the system repeatedly at four resolutions, 

Nx = 20 , 40 , 80 , 160 , and perform forward validation for each feedback. We report grid-by-grid changes in the value function, control field, and cascade probability. The killing-parameter sensitivity experiment scans ő≥ ‚ąą { 5, 10 , 15 , 20 } and őĽbase ‚ąą { 0.01 , 0.03 , 0.05 , 0.10 } to test how the soft killing 16 mechanism changes tail loss levels in high-pressure and near-saturation regions. The external-data calibration experiments use two categories of public regulatory data. The first is the EBA 2024 EU-wide Transparency Exercise data, used to extract bank-level asset-size proxies, risk-exposure-amount proxies, institutional jurisdictions, and country-level credit-exposure proxies. The second is the official reporting package from the EBA Pillar 3 Data Hub / European Data Access Portal, used to record institution-level regulatory disclosure modules and reporting-file structures. These public data constrain bank size distributions, risk-exposure proxies, jurisdictional structure, and disclosure coverage. We use them to construct EBA and Pillar 3 calibrated synthetic core‚Äď periphery networks, with bilateral edges generated inside those public constraints. The jurisdictional distribution is visualized using a public country-boundary base map for geographic presentation. In addition to a single representative network, we fix the public-data constraints and independently generate multiple calibrated synthetic networks, using the cross-instance distribution to test the stability of the control conclusions across unobserved bilateral edge realizations. 

# 9 Finite-type matrix experiments 

The finite-type matrix system exhibits a moderate but clear near-critical transition under the moderate-stress specification. Without control, as the matrix scaling increases from 45 to 50 , the terminal mean loss, tail loss, and cascade probability increase together. Figure 1 reports this transition. The result shows that the contagion matrix is not a mere calibration parameter, but a structural variable that organizes systemic loss paths. (a) Matrix scaling (b) Risk-cost frontier 

Figure 1: Finite-type matrix scaling and risk‚Äďcost frontier in the near-critical region. The control experiments show that different rules form a risk‚Äďcost frontier in the same near-critical region. Risk-threshold control gives the lowest terminal loss and tail loss, but has the highest control cost. Uniform control is a strong benchmark. Type-priority control and contagion-pressure threshold control leave more residual loss but require lower intervention cost. Figure 1 shows that the control conclusion is not global dominance of one strategy over all others. Different information structures and control intensities correspond to different positions on the frontier. The quantitative convergence result in Theorem 2 is numerically tested in Figure 2. The horizontal axis is the minimum particle number across types, N‚ąó. The vertical axes are the terminal loss MSE and the one-dimensional empirical W 22 diagnostic of the lifted empirical measure. The theorem itself is stated in the bounded-Lipschitz metric, while the empirical W 22 panel is reported as a numerical stability diagnostic for the same first-order particle scale. The curves for all types have the same order as the CT /N ‚ąó reference line, showing that the finite-particle system is consistent with 17 the N ‚ąí1/2 

> ‚ąó

error scale predicted by the theory. Table 1 reports log‚Äďlog slopes and fitted constants. Slopes near ‚ąí1 correspond to the N ‚ąí1/2

# 10 Matrix approximation boundary for sparse graphs 

Figure 3 first gives structural diagnostics for the three sparse network classes. The adjacency sparsity pattern of the stochastic block network is close to within-block uniform sampling. The core‚Äďperiphery network forms denser local connections around core targets and core sources. The heavy-tailed network generates stronger node-level concentration through hub exposure and the upper tail of weighted in-exposure. Figure 4 shows the same differences through representative subgraph layouts. These figures are not additional modeling assumptions. They provide structural background for the matrix approximation gaps studied below: the aggregate matrix preserves average exposure across types, but compresses within-block row-exposure dispersion, edge concentration, and local neighborhood shape. Figure 5 reports cascade probabilities for the three sparse network classes under the exposure multiplier sweep. The heavy-tailed network enters the graph cascade region first, followed by the core‚Äďperiphery network, while the stochastic block network enters last. In these near-critical regions, the corresponding aggregate matrix approximations still have low cascade probabilities. Cascades in 18 0 600 1200 1800

Figure 5: Sparse graph systems versus aggregate matrix approximations under exposure scaling. The tail-loss gap is clearer in Figure 5. As the exposure multiplier increases, the tail-loss gap of the graph system relative to the matrix approximation expands rapidly. The gap appears earlier in the heavy-tailed and core‚Äďperiphery networks, showing that locally concentrated exposure can worsen tail paths before aggregate matrix risk indicators raise an alarm. Figure 6 converts this pattern into critical exposure multipliers and includes Barabasi‚ÄďAlbert scale-free networks and Watts‚ÄďStrogatz small-world networks in the same comparison. The heavy-tailed network requires the lowest multiplier to reach a 10% graph cascade probability, and the core‚Äďperiphery network enters the critical region next. The small-world network, stochastic block network, and scale-free network require higher exposure multipliers to reach the same threshold. This five-network ordering shows that local exposure concentration, core neighborhoods, and clustering structure all shift the systemic cascade boundary, while the type-aggregated matrix cannot fully capture these differences from average exposure alone. 0 5 10 15 20 25

Figure 6: Exposure multipliers at which different network structures reach the graph cascade threshold. 20 Three representative near-critical points make the approximation boundary concrete. In the heavy-tailed network at multiplier 12 , graph tail loss is 0.3637 and matrix tail loss is 0.0798 , giving a tail gap of 0.2838 , while graph cascade probability is 0.5000 and matrix cascade probability remains 0. In the core‚Äďperiphery network at multiplier 15 , the corresponding tail gap is 0.5373 and graph cascade probability is 0.4800 ; in the stochastic-block network at multiplier 26 , the tail gap is 0.2914 and graph cascade probability is 0.4000 . These three points are the main evidence for the approximation boundary: the type-aggregated matrix of the same graph can preserve average exposure, but it cannot fully preserve the tail paths generated by locally concentrated exposure. To test whether the transition ordering depends on Monte Carlo error, we re-estimate the three network classes with 500 replications on the key interval x = 8 , . . . , 16 . Figure 7 gives confidence bands for graph-system cascade probabilities and tail gaps. The refined estimates preserve the same ordering: the heavy-tailed network first enters the cascade region at exposure multiplier 11 

with maximum tail gap 0.796 and maximum cascade probability 1.000 ; the core‚Äďperiphery network reaches the 10% cascade threshold at multiplier 14 with maximum tail gap 0.544 and maximum cascade probability 0.940 ; and the stochastic block network still does not reach a 10% cascade probability on this interval, with maximum tail gap only 0.037 . The differences across the three network classes are therefore stable transition differences caused by local exposure concentration, rather than sampling fluctuations from a small number of replications. 8 9 10 11 12 13 14 15 16

The stochastic-block network reaches its largest mean gap at multiplier 30 , where the mean gap is 0.3973 , the tail gap is 0.5228 , and DT3 (A) = 41 .9001 . These values show that the structural diagnostic is conservative in level but separates the high-gap heterogeneous networks from the more regular stochastic-block benchmark. For the core‚Äďperiphery and heavy-tailed graphs, the plotted DT3 (A) is a conditional structural diagnostic under the within-type sampling regularity in Theorem 3. We also rescale the three network classes to the same aggregate spectral radius ŌĀ(Őā őď) = 5 to separate total contagion amplification from local aggregation error. Figure 8 shows that, under this common aggregate-amplification scale, the realized gaps in this calibration become small and comparable. The mean gaps are 0.0055 for the heavy-tailed network, 0.0048 for the core‚Äďperiphery network, and 0.0054 for the stochastic-block network; the corresponding tail gaps are 0.0063 , 0.0061 ,and 0.0069 . The fixed-spectral-radius diagnostics are also close, with DT3 (A) = 9 .7896 , 8.5289 ,and 9.8056 , respectively. This comparison first normalizes aggregate feedback strength and then reads the residual differences through the local row-dispersion and square-edge-spread channels in Theorem 3. The common-noise experiment brings the case ŌÉ0 > 0 from the theory into the sparse graph numerical analysis. Figure 9 shows that, with 500 replications, terminal tail loss and the matrix approximation gap rise overall as common-noise intensity increases, especially around ŌÉ0 = 0 .15 

and 0.20 . Across ŌÉ0 ‚ąą { 0, 0.05 , 0.10 , 0.15 , 0.20 }, no-control 95% loss ranges from 0.385 to 0.448 ,graph-pressure 95% loss ranges from 0.238 to 0.297 , and cascade reduction per unit cost remains between 16 .41 and 20 .77 . Common shocks move the safety margins of different nodes in the same direction and therefore amplify synchronization in local network pressure, but they do not eliminate the value of local pressure information. 

# 11 Graph-pressure threshold control and control transfer 

The matrix approximation boundary itself concerns risk identification. The next question is whether local pressure information in the sparse graph can support more effective control when the aggregate matrix misses local cascades. We therefore compare no control, type-uniform control, and graph-pressure threshold control at three representative points. Graph-pressure threshold control triggers intervention according to node-level local pressure in (6) , and can therefore react to locally high-pressure nodes. Because type-uniform control and graph-pressure threshold control naturally operate at different cost levels, we also repeat the comparison under fixed budgets. Figure 12 gives the cascade probability frontier under budget constraints. Graph-pressure threshold control usually achieves substantial cascade reduction at lower cost in the low-budget region. versus 0.170 in the heavy-tailed network, and 0.035 versus 0.065 in the stochastic-block network. 0.151 and 95% loss around 0.085 ‚Äď0.088 at budget 0.200 . At budgets 0.100 and 0.200 , type-uniform control reaches the strong-suppression regime with zero observed cascade probability in most cases and substantially larger realized costs, while graph-pressure control remains a low-cost frontier point with positive residual risk. 

# 12 Two-population optimal control numerical loop 

Theorem 6 gives the HJB characterization, within the lifted comparison framework, and the smooth-verification first-order condition for the controlled McKean‚ÄďVlasov system with K = 2 

under common noise. To connect this theoretical structure to the numerical analysis, we solve 24 0.025 0.050 0.075 0.100 0.125 0.150 0.175 0.200

Figure 12: Cascade probability comparison between type-uniform control and graph-pressure threshold control under fixed budgets. a finite-dimensional projection HJB on the state (x1, x 2, L 1, L 2), with controls taking values in 

[0 , ¬Įa]2. The discretization uses a semi-implicit step for the second-order diffusion terms in the two x directions to avoid numerical drift caused by the explicit diffusion CFL condition on fine grids. The common-noise cross term ŌÉ20 ‚ąā2 

> x1x2

V is retained explicitly. The killing term corresponds to a one-way upward jump to the default cemetery state and the loss coordinates. Numerically, we approximate V (L + ‚ąÜ L) ‚ąí V (L) using a semi-Lagrangian loss-jump scheme, which preserves monotonicity in the loss direction and reduces the ill-conditioned extrapolation caused by central differences or coarse-grid projection. The resulting feedback control is then used in a forward K = 2 

core‚Äďperiphery particle system. The projected first-order control formula is evaluated on this compact finite-difference state grid. On the interior grid points, the semi-implicit diffusion operator is nondegenerate in the alive-state coordinates and the drift, killing, and interpolation coefficients are bounded Lipschitz; the stored value surface is evaluated through the same smooth finite-difference and monotone interpolation operators used in the Hamiltonian step. Thus the numerical minimization is a discrete smooth-verification analogue of the formula in Theorem 6. Projection onto [0 , ¬Įa]2 handles the saturation boundary, and nonsmooth grid points are handled by direct projected Hamiltonian minimization. The HJB risk‚Äďcost comparison places the HJB feedback point, no control, type-uniform control, and graph-pressure threshold control on the same metrics. This finite-dimensional discretization shows that the Hamiltonian structure in Theorem 6 generates computable feedback and can be compared with rule-based controls. In the same forward K = 2 system, no control and the representative two-population graph-pressure threshold control both have mean terminal loss about 

0.362 , 95% loss about 0.375 , and cascade probability 1.000 . Type-uniform control reduces the 95% loss to 0.211 and cascade probability to 0.455 at cost 0.0143 . The finite-dimensional HJB feedback further reduces the 95% loss to 0.202 and cascade probability to 0.095 at cost 0.0162 . Thus, in this low-dimensional projection, the HJB feedback achieves lower tail loss and lower cascade probability at a comparable intervention scale. Figure 13 reports the multi-grid diagnostics under the semi-Lagrangian loss-jump scheme. The four resolutions use the same calibration and terminal penalty, and each feedback is evaluated in the 25 same K = 2 forward system. As the grid is refined from Nx = 20 to Nx = 160 , the initial value V0

decreases from 0.1713 to 0.0459 , the initial control decreases from (1 .625 , 1.750) to (0 .625 , 0.625) , and the average control cost decreases from 0.0680 to 0.0087 . Forward tail loss and cascade probability still change sharply, with cascade probability rising from 0 on the coarse grid to 0.790 and 1.000 .These grid diagnostics track the finite-dimensional feedback approximation, while the forward validation metrics below provide the primary policy-performance test. 20 40 60 80 100 120 140 160

Figure 13: K = 2 HJB multi-grid and Richardson-type diagnostics. The Richardson-type panel in Figure 13 gives a power-law diagnostic for the four available grids. The estimated exponent is p ‚ČÉ 0.476 , the fitted R2 is close to 1, and the extrapolated intercept is negative. This value identifies the dominant discretization effect: when NL = Nx/2, the semi-Lagrangian term V (L + ‚ąÜ L) ‚ąí V (L) still smears the jump discontinuity created by killing and loss advancement. We use the panel as a discretization-sensitivity diagnostic and evaluate policy performance through direct forward simulation. To separate diffusion discretization in the x directions from loss-coordinate projection error, we fix Nx = 320 and compare NL = 20 , 40 , 60 , 80 . Figure 14 shows that V0 decreases from 0.1158 

to 0.0460 , the control cost decreases from 0.0400 to 0.0099 , and the forward cascade probability rises from 0 to 0.610 and 1.000 . Since the second-order HJB terms act only in the x directions, this pattern locates the dominant sensitivity in the loss-coordinate projection and the killing-jump interpolation. 

Fixed-loss slice diagnostic The fixed-loss slice diagnostic fixes L1 = L2 = 0 and refines only the 

(x1, x 2) plane. The HJB initial value is 0.3134 at Nx = 320 and 0.3137 at Nx = 640 , with average slice controls about 1.641 on both grids. Figure 15 reports the corresponding control slices. This confirms that the dominant four-dimensional feedback sensitivity comes from loss-coordinate projection and killing-jump interpolation; diffusion discretization in the alive coordinates is comparatively stable on this slice.

Figure 17: Time slices of K = 2 HJB feedback control at t = 0 , 0.25 , 0.50 , 0.75 .Graph-pressure threshold control in the two-population projection requires a separate explanation. In the representative-state HJB comparison, the graph-pressure variable is generated by type-average new losses, so local pressure heterogeneity within the same type is projected out. The threshold 28 rule only observes two representative pressure coordinates and cannot identify differences between high-pressure and low-pressure nodes in the sparse graph. The information value conclusion in Theorem 7 requires node-level local pressure information to be strictly finer than type-average information. The representative K = 2 projection therefore illustrates the mechanism cleanly: the value of local pressure information comes from node-level information refinement inside a type, not from the label assigned to a type-average pressure coordinate. To test this interpretation, we recalibrate graph-pressure threshold control in a large-scale 

K = 2 forward system. This experiment restores local pressure heterogeneity in the core‚Äďperiphery system, so the threshold parameters can form nontrivial risk‚Äďcost points. Together with the representative projection above, it shows that the local pressure rule becomes effective precisely when the information domain contains node-level pressure differences.

0.205 , and cost 0.0300 . The HJB feedback attains 95% loss 0.2034 , cascade probability 0.120 , and cost 0.0166 , indicating that the HJB feedback reaches comparable tail loss with lower cost and lower cascade probability. The threshold grid covers seven values from 0.0075 to 0.0300 , and the control upper-bound ratios are 0.70 , 1.00 , 1.30 . Each parameter point uses 500 Monte Carlo replications. Peak graph-pressure efficiency across network families The parameter points with the highest cascade reduction per unit cost show that graph-pressure thresholds reduce cascade risk at low cost across the three benchmark networks and the two additional degree-distribution networks. The efficiency ranking remains largest in locally heterogeneous networks: heavy-tailed, core‚Äďperiphery, and stochastic-block networks attain high-efficiency points, Barab√°si‚ÄďAlbert networks are intermediate, and Watts‚ÄďStrogatz networks are lowest. This ordering is consistent with the mechanism that local pressure information becomes more valuable when exposure neighborhoods are uneven. The highest-efficiency and lowest-residual-cascade parameter points are separated in a stable way. In the heavy-tailed network, the highest-efficiency point uses threshold 0.0300 and upper-bound ratio 0.7000 , with cascade probability 0.278 and cost 0.0117 ; the lowest-cascade point uses threshold 

0.0075 and ratio 1.3000 , with cascade probability 0.054 and cost 0.0445 . The corresponding pairs are (0 .256 , 0.0123) versus (0 .046 , 0.0471) for the core‚Äďperiphery network, and (0 .166 , 0.0120) versus 

(0 .016 , 0.0426) for the stochastic-block network. High-efficiency points therefore use lower control upper bounds, retain some residual cascades, and have very low costs, while the lowest-cascade points require higher upper bounds and higher costs. Parameter robustness analysis reveals the continuous shape of the risk‚Äďcost frontier more clearly than a single representative point. 30 We also conduct network heterogeneity comparisons under fixed spectral radius and fixed average weighted in-exposure to separate total exposure intensity from local exposure concentration. These comparisons standardize the aggregate matrix spectral radius or the average weighted in-exposure of different networks to the same level and then compare the graph system with the matrix system again. The results support our structural interpretation: after controlling for aggregate intensity, heterogeneous networks still display a stronger tendency toward tail gaps. Matrix approximation gaps therefore arise not only from amplification of total edge weight, but also from the concentration pattern of edge weights at the node level. The time-window robustness comparison extends the same sparse graph transition-region experiment to T = 2 .0. The longer time window moves all three network classes into the cascade region earlier: the core‚Äďperiphery and heavy-tailed networks both reach a 10% cascade probability at exposure multiplier 8, with maximum tail gaps 0.722 and 0.658 , respectively, while the stochastic-block network enters the cascade region at multiplier 14 with maximum tail gap 0.151 . The time window mainly shifts the cascade onset, while local exposure concentration and matrix approximation tail gaps still determine the relative ordering across networks. 8 9 10 11 12 13 14 15 16

The results show that time-varying resampling changes the arrival paths of local pressure and the magnitude of tail loss, but the relative ordering across the three network classes and the value of local pressure information remain stable. Matrix approximation gaps therefore mainly come from exposure heterogeneity and local pressure information, with the network-ordering result stable across static and time-varying edge realizations. Quantitatively, the static and time-varying comparisons remain close. In the heavy-tailed network, no-control cascade probabilities are 0.715 and 0.710 under static and time-varying edge sets, while graph-pressure control gives 0.145 and 0.170 . In the core‚Äďperiphery and stochastic-block networks, graph-pressure control keeps cascade probability at zero in both regimes, with 95% losses changing only modestly. The time-varying experiment therefore perturbs path details without changing the network ordering. Figure 22 further adds Barab√°si‚ÄďAlbert scale-free networks and Watts‚ÄďStrogatz small-world networks. The former emphasizes the upper tail of the source-node degree distribution, while the latter emphasizes local clustering and short paths. These two additional network classes do not change the main mechanism. In the cascade onset region, larger structural concentration is accompanied by a larger graph‚Äďmatrix tail gap. In extremely high-exposure regions, some matrix systems also enter high-loss states, and the gap contracts because the two systems saturate at the same time. This result shows that row dispersion and edge concentration apply not only to the three benchmark networks used in this paper, but also to the onset conditions for matrix approximation distortion under more general degree distributions. 8 9 10 11 12 13 14 15 16

Numerical scale of the information-value constants Several constants in the information-value lower bound of Theorem 7 are informative as scale diagnostics. Here qk is estimated from type terminal losses in the uncontrolled forward system, the matrix-approximation concentration score is computed from the adjacency matrix, and mk is estimated from the marginal elasticity of the kill 33 intensity with respect to control near the initial state. The estimated lower-bound scales are 0.1830 

for the heavy-tailed network, 0.0754 for the core‚Äďperiphery network, 0.0362 for the Barab√°si‚ÄďAlbert network, 0.0301 for the stochastic-block network, and 0.0293 for the Watts‚ÄďStrogatz network. This ordering is used as a structural scale check rather than as an exact prediction formula, and it is consistent with the interpretation that local pressure information has greater value when within-block exposure heterogeneity is larger. The large-scale sparse experiment extends the sparse graph experiments to a larger particle scale. The three network classes preserve the same ordering at the larger scale: heavy-tailed and core‚Äď periphery structures show tail gaps earlier, while the gap in the stochastic block network appears later. This result shows that the matrix approximation boundary and the value of graph-pressure threshold control are not small-simulation artifacts and continue to appear in larger sparse systems. At N = 50 ,000 and multiplier 30 , all three benchmark networks reach cascade probability 1.000 .The 95% losses are 0.9842 for the core‚Äďperiphery network, 0.9939 for the heavy-tailed network, and 

0.5486 for the stochastic-block network, with corresponding tail gaps 0.4331 , 0.0720 , and 0.4734 .The large-scale check therefore preserves the qualitative boundary: the graph system remains substantially more fragile than its type-aggregated matrix approximation in heterogeneous sparse regimes. K = 3 and K = 4 . The K = 3 specification merges small banks and regional banks into the periphery type, while the K = 4 specification keeps the original four-type structure. In both dimensions, the matrix spectral radius and scaling parameter organize the increase in terminal mean loss and tail loss. # 14 External-data calibrated network 

We combine the preceding synthetic network mechanism with public regulatory data to construct a semi-realistic calibrated core‚Äďperiphery network. The calibration inputs come from the EBA 2024 EU-wide Transparency Exercise and the official disclosure packages of the EBA Pillar 3 Data Hub / European Data Access Portal [ 60 , 61 ]. The transparency data provide bank-level 34 Table 2: Finite-type matrix experiments for K = 3 and K = 4 .

size, risk-exposure amount, and jurisdictional information. The Pillar 3/EDAP disclosure packages provide institution-level regulatory disclosure modules and reporting-file structures. Together, they constrain the size concentration, core-node share, country-level credit-exposure proxies, and disclosure coverage structure in the synthetic network. The jurisdictional map uses the Natural Earth country-boundary base map [ 62 ], which is used only to visualize country outlines and does not provide financial exposure information. Public EBA and Pillar 3 data do not identify bank-to-bank bilateral exposures. We therefore construct a calibrated synthetic core‚Äďperiphery network that is compatible with public regulatory statistics. Specifically, nodes are sampled according to EBA asset-size proxies, core nodes are determined by the upper tail of the size distribution, row exposure budgets are adjusted by institution size and country-level credit-exposure proxies, and a sparse core‚Äďperiphery adjacency matrix is then generated with the aggregate matrix spectral radius rescaled to ŌĀ(Őāőď) ‚ČÉ 5. We use this network to test whether our mechanisms for the matrix approximation boundary and graph-pressure threshold control continue to appear in a calibrated environment constrained by external data. Core nodes are determined by the upper tail of the size distribution, so the core share comes from public size constraints. Asset concentration in the calibrated core‚Äďperiphery partition The calibrated sample contains 112 EBA reporting banks and 27 bank jurisdictions, and the synthetic network contains 10000 nodes and about 711000 directed weighted edges. Core nodes represent 8.04% of institutions but 47.78% of total asset-size proxy mass; periphery nodes represent 91.96% of institutions and 52.22% of the proxy mass. The core‚Äďperiphery split therefore comes from the upper tail of the size distribution rather than from arbitrary assignment, and the row-dispersion and edge-concentration diagnostics show that node-level exposure concentration remains relevant even after total contagion strength is controlled through the spectral radius. Figure 25 reports forward risk results on this calibrated network. Under the near-critical stress specification, the uncontrolled graph system has terminal cascade probability 0.486 and mean terminal loss 0.2000 . Graph-pressure threshold control lowers the cascade probability to 0.174 at control cost 0.0227 . Type-uniform control further eliminates the observed cascade events, but its cost rises to 0.2195 . This ordering is consistent with the fixed-budget and sparse graph control results above: local pressure information has substantial marginal value in the low-cost region, while uniform control is a high-cost strong-suppression benchmark. Figure 26 shows the average cumulative loss paths under the three control rules. The no-control path crosses the neighborhood of the cascade threshold in the middle-to-late part of the horizon. Graph-pressure threshold control reduces the path slope and suppresses tail paths. Uniform control produces stronger overall suppression at higher cost. # 15 Discussion and conclusion 

The results identify a clear effective range for finite-type matrix approximation. In low-pressure or regular-mixing regions, the aggregate contagion matrix gives a useful stress-testing object because it preserves the main type-level exposure channels and supports interpretable spectral and row-exposure diagnostics. As local exposure concentration rises, the same matrix can preserve aggregate exposure while missing tail paths through hubs, core nodes, or high-pressure neighborhoods. The diagnostic terms in Theorem 3, the tail-gap construction in Theorem 4, and the spectral threshold in Theorem 5 describe this boundary from complementary angles. The control experiments translate the approximation boundary into regulatory design. Type-uniform control is a strong benchmark when the intervention budget is large and broad suppression is feasible. Graph-pressure threshold control uses node-level local pressure and therefore performs best when the budget is limited and the cascade mechanism is concentrated in a small set of high-pressure neighborhoods. The finite-dimensional K = 2 HJB loop provides a marginal-value benchmark: it converts the Hamiltonian structure into a feedback rule that reduces tail loss and cascade probability at comparable cost. The forward policy evaluation applies the stored feedback in a large particle system and serves as the primary validation metric for the computed control. The HJB diagnostics also clarify the meaning of the Richardson-style extrapolation. A negative extrapolated value such as V‚ąě = ‚ąí0.029 identifies a finite-dimensional projection effect: the loss-coordinate grid smears the jump discontinuity created when killing transfers alive mass into the cemetery state and advances the loss coordinates near the contagion threshold. The representative-coordinate projection also suppresses within-type distributional heterogeneity that the full measure-valued HJB would retain. The semi-Lagrangian loss-jump scheme reduces the instability of central differences, and the remaining loss-grid sensitivity is reported through the grid diagnostics. The value-function grid tests discretization sensitivity, while the forward controlled particle system tests realized risk reduction by the computed feedback. 38 The external-data layer gives calibrated synthetic evidence under public regulatory constraints. EBA Transparency Exercise data and Pillar 3/EDAP disclosure packages constrain size, jurisdictional distribution, concentration, and disclosure coverage, while the confidential bilateral exposure matrix remains unobserved. The calibrated core‚Äďperiphery networks use these public constraints to test robustness across admissible synthetic edge realizations. The multi-instance results show that the matrix-approximation gap and the value of local pressure information are stable across network draws satisfying the same external constraints. 

Open problems Four directions follow naturally from the present framework: sparse-graph McKean‚ÄďVlasov limits with killing jumps, common noise, and local weak convergence; scalable feedback classes based on neighborhood distributions or graph neural networks; comparison theory for degenerate, hard-boundary, or unbounded-intensity versions beyond the bounded killed HJB treated here; and richer calibration pipelines using supervisory bilateral data, when available, to separate model error from disclosure-driven network uncertainty. The central conclusion is that finite-type matrices are informative stress-testing tools in regular regimes, while heterogeneous sparse networks require local diagnostics for tail-risk measurement and intervention. 

# Code and data availability 

Replication code is available at https://github.com/Hopper-221/systemic_mkv . The repository contains the forward simulation, finite-dimensional HJB feedback, calibrated synthetic-network construction, and figure/table generation scripts used for the numerical experiments. Public regulatory inputs are obtained from the EBA Transparency Exercise and Pillar 3 Data Hub and are used to construct calibrated synthetic core‚Äďperiphery networks under public disclosure constraints. 39 SM1 Numerical discretization and parameter calibration 

This appendix records two key discretization choices used in the numerical experiments in the main text. First, the two-population HJB in Theorem 6 is projected onto the representative state (x1, x 2, L 1, L 2) and solved by backward induction from T to 0. The first-order terms in the 

x directions use central differences. The two pure second-order diffusion terms are handled by semi-implicit tridiagonal steps, and the common-noise cross second-order term ŌÉ20 ‚ąā2 

> x1x2

V is retained explicitly. The control is searched on a discrete grid over the compact set [0 , ¬Įa]2. The killing term enters the loss coordinates through a one-way semi-Lagrangian jump, using monotone piecewise-linear interpolation in the loss coordinates to approximate V (x1, x 2, L 1 +‚ąÜ L1, L 2)‚ąíV (x1, x 2, L 1, L 2)

and the corresponding term for the second type. This preserves the direction in which losses can only increase and reduces ill-conditioned extrapolation caused by central differences or coarse-grid projection. The explicit treatment of the cross term is used only in the calibrated range of common-noise intensities considered in the experiments; the time step and grid are checked through the multi-grid diagnostics, which serve as an ex post CFL-type stability check for this finite-dimensional projection. The multi-grid diagnostic tables in the main text repeat this discretization at 

Nx = 20 , 40 , 80 , 160 and NL = Nx/2, and validate the feedback control in the same forward system by its cascade probability. This diagnostic identifies the sensitivity of finite-dimensional feedback to grid resolution. The rigorous value-function characterization is still given by the measure-valued HJB in Theorem 6 and Appendix SM4. Second, the K = 2 numerical experiments must lie in a nondegenerate near-critical region: if pressure is too low, the marginal control benefit in the Hamiltonian is zero; if pressure is too high, all rules enter the saturated-control region. We scan the kill-intensity multiplier, the terminal-loss weight multiplier, and the running-cost multiplier on a small grid, and choose a parameter region that satisfies three conditions at once: the uncontrolled system has significant cascade risk, HJB feedback gives nonzero control, and the HJB point and rule-based controls have comparable cost magnitudes. Figure 28 and Table 3 give the numerical basis for this calibration. The final calibration used in the main text is kill-intensity multiplier 25 , terminal-loss weight multiplier 6, and running-cost multiplier 0.05 .

Computational environment and runtime 

The numerical experiments were carried out on a Linux workstation equipped with two AMD EPYC 7542 processors, 64 physical CPU cores, 128 hardware threads, 128 GB of system memory, and one NVIDIA GeForce RTX 4090 GPU with 24 GB of memory. The software environment used Python 3.12.7 together with NumPy 1.26.4, SciPy 1.13.1, pandas 2.2.2, Matplotlib 3.9.2, and a development build of PyTorch 2.8.0 with CUDA 12.8 support. The finite-difference HJB computations and sparse-network Monte Carlo experiments were implemented as CPU-based NumPy/SciPy workloads. Although the workstation also contained an NVIDIA RTX 4090 GPU, the reported results do not rely on GPU execution. The final numerical campaign required approximately 145 aggregate wall-clock job-hours, covering the sparse-control robustness recomputation, the non-HJB numerical experiments, the monotone-jump and semi-Lagrangian HJB computations, and the additional Watts‚ÄďStrogatz and Barab√°si‚Äď Albert robustness checks. Random seeds were fixed before each experiment family was run. The principal sparse-graph, matrix-approximation, Theorem 3 diagnostic, and two-type HJB experiments use base seed 2026. The later robustness checks, EBA/Pillar 3 calibration experiments, multi-instance experiments, and auxiliary network-family experiments use fixed seeds recorded with the corresponding experimental outputs. Across all experiment families, seeds were specified ex ante 40 and were not selected based on numerical outcomes.

mean-square estimate used in Theorem SM2.7. Proof. Condition on FW 0 

> T
