The common-noise adapted control is then a deterministic path, and the remaining randomness comes only from the independent initial states, idiosyncratic Brownian motions, and Poisson thinning measures. Hence the limiting killed paths are i.i.d. on the path space D([0 , T ]; E), with E = R ‚ą™ { ‚ąā}. The bounded-Lipschitz unit ball on this Polish path space has a countable determining subclass. The ordinary Glivenko‚ÄďCantelli theorem for this countable determining class, followed by monotone approximation of the time supremum over a dense grid and the no-fixed-atom property of the killing times, gives the two uniform convergence statements after 43 removing the conditioning. The quadratic version is the standard empirical-process strengthening of the same conditional LLN and is stated separately in Theorem SM2.7 because it is the finite-sample input needed for the N ‚ąí1 

> k

rate.

The centered compensator martingale has quadratic variation bounded by C/N k, because the intensity is bounded by őõ. The same entropy maximal inequality transfers these pointwise estimates to the bounded-Lipschitz supremum. This yields Lemma SM2.2 under the stated finite-entropy condition. 

Remark SM2.4 (Concrete localized entropy verification) . In the compact alive-state version used for the bounded-killing theory, the finite-entropy condition in Lemma SM2.3 can be checked from standard bracketing entropy bounds. After adjoining the isolated cemetery point, the lifted killed-path space is the union of a compact one-dimensional diffusion path class with one cemetery jump coordinate. The bounded drift, bounded diffusion coefficient, and bounded killing rate give a uniform modulus-in-probability for the alive diffusion paths, and the cemetery coordinate is a monotone indicator with no fixed-time atom. The bounded-Lipschitz unit ball restricted to this localized class has polynomial bracketing entropy under the laws generated by the model; hence the Dudley‚Äďbracketing integral is finite by the entropy integral criterion of van der Vaart and Wellner [ 63 , Theorem 2.7.11]. Therefore, on compact state grids or after localization before removing the localization error, the quadratic empirical input in Theorem SM2.7 follows from the stated bounded-Lipschitz and bounded-killing primitives. 

Remark SM2.5 (Empirical-process input for the conditional rate) . The quadratic empirical inputs in Theorem SM2.7 are verified by Lemma SM2.3 under a localized finite-entropy condition, and are otherwise stated explicitly because the killed-particle state space is path-valued and contains a cemetery state. They are standard once one works conditionally on the common-noise path. Conditional on FW 0 

> T

, the limiting killed paths are i.i.d. random elements of the Polish space 

D([0 , T ]; E), with E = R ‚ą™ { ‚ąā}. The bounded-Lipschitz unit ball on this space admits a countable determining subclass, and the usual separability and symmetrization arguments for empirical processes give the N ‚ąí1 

> k

second-moment fluctuation scale for bounded Lipschitz test functions; see, for example, van der Vaart and Wellner [ 63 ]. The additional metric-level stability term in Lemma SM2.2 is the synchronous Poisson-thinning analogue of the same argument: the alive-state displacement, cemetery-state mismatch, and compensator martingale terms are controlled before taking the bounded-Lipschitz supremum. Thus Theorem SM2.7 should be read as a conditional quantitative propagation estimate under these standard empirical-process and thinning-stability inputs, not as a pointwise-test-function estimate upgraded without justification.

# SM3 Proofs for the matrix approximation boundary, tail gap, and spectral-radius threshold 

Matched time-grid coupling convention The graph‚Äďmatrix comparison in Theorem SM3.4 is stated for the same discrete time grid used by the numerical system, 

0 = t0 < t 1 < ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ < t M = T, ‚ąÜtm = tm ‚ąí tm‚ąí1.

The graph system and matrix system are synchronously coupled: they use the same initial states, Brownian increments, common noise increments, and killing randomization variables. At each step, the state is first advanced by drift and diffusion to a pre-contagion value. New defaults are then sampled from the state-dependent killing probability 

1 ‚ąí exp {‚ąí őĽg(i)(Xpre

Thus Assumption SM3.1 says that the within-block default set does not generate concentration fluctuations larger than the square-edge scale. It is the local uniform sampling condition needed for matrix approximation, not a circular assumption inferred from the matrix approximation to be proved. In core‚Äďperiphery and heavy-tailed networks this condition is not automatic; in those regimes the theorem should be read as a diagnostic bound conditional on the stated sampling regularity, while the numerical experiments separately measure how large the row-dispersion and square-edge-spread terms become when local concentration is strong.

Remark SM3.7 (Feasibility of the separation condition) . Condition (A) is a separation condition requiring the tail synchronous killing effect to dominate the effect of uniformly dispersed exposure. This condition is not vacuous. Given ŌĀ > q > 0.05 , if the share of type-one nodes is small and the share of shocked type-two nodes is large, then ŌÄ1ŌĀ can be made arbitrarily small. For example, take 

ŌÄ1 = 0 .05 , ŌÄ2 = 0 .95 , q = 0 .06 , and ŌĀ = 0 .08 . Then it suffices that 

p+ ‚ąí p‚ąí > ŌÄ1ŌĀŌÄ2

‚Čą 0.00421 .

When the type-two kill intensity is sufficiently steep near the risk boundary, the difference in kill probabilities induced by pressures a and ŌĀa , namely p+ ‚ąí p‚ąí, can strictly exceed this threshold. Thus one default of the hub node can push many type-two nodes into the high-kill region with probability greater than 5% , while the same aggregate exposure in the homogeneous network remains controlled at the 0.95 tail by dispersion. 

Corollary SM3.8 (Embedding the tail-gap construction in larger finite type systems) . Let K ‚Č• 2,and choose two distinct active types s and r with asymptotic shares ŌÄs, ŌÄ r > 0. Suppose the two-type separation condition in Theorem SM3.6 holds after replacing (ŌÄ1, ŌÄ 2) by (ŌÄs, ŌÄ r) and using the source type s and target type r. On the remaining type blocks, assume either that the common terminal-loss contribution is deterministic, or more generally that it is coupled identically in the hub and homogeneous graph sequences, is independent of the active (r, s ) construction, and has no atom at the shifted 0.95 quantile used in the active-block comparison. Then there exist two K-type graph sequences with the same type assignment, the same aggregate matrix, and identical dynamics on all type blocks except the block (r, s ), such that their 0.95 terminal-loss quantiles differ by at least a positive constant for all sufficiently large n.

65 Proof. Embed the source set Sn of Theorem SM3.6 inside type s and the target set Rn inside type 

r. On the block (r, s ) use the hub and homogeneous edge constructions from the theorem. On all remaining type blocks use the same edge weights in the two graph sequences; for instance, take these blocks to be zero and choose their initial states so that they contribute deterministic zero terminal loss in both systems. The type-aggregated matrices remain identical because the only modified block has the same block average in the hub and homogeneous constructions. If the inactive blocks contribute deterministic zero terminal loss, they do not affect the positive separation created on the active (r, s ) block. In the more general case, their common terminal-loss contribution shifts both terminal-loss distributions by the same independent component. The no-atom condition at the shifted quantile preserves the strict quantile separation after a possible reduction of the margin. The proof of Theorem SM3.6, with (ŌÄs, ŌÄ r) replacing (ŌÄ1, ŌÄ 2) and with the resulting shifted quantile margin, gives the stated positive quantile gap. 

Remark SM3.9 (Robustness of the embedding) . The embedding does not rely on all other types having zero loss. The same conclusion holds if the inactive types have nonzero terminal losses that are coupled identically in the hub and homogeneous graph sequences and are independent of the active (r, s ) tail construction, or more generally if their common contribution has no atom at the relevant shifted quantile. In that case the inactive-type losses shift both terminal-loss distributions by the same common component, and the strict separation condition in Theorem SM3.6 is adjusted only through the resulting type-share and quantile-margin constants. This turns the two-type construction into a robust local block embedding rather than a purely zero-background example. 

Remark SM3.10 (No-atom condition in finite simulations) . The no-atom condition in Corollary SM3.8 is a continuous-time or large-population regularity condition. In a finite-particle discrete-time simulation, terminal losses take values on a finite grid and hence have atoms. The condition holds generically after an arbitrarily small continuous perturbation of initial states, idiosyncratic shocks, or terminal observation time; the limiting continuous-time construction is the object used in the corollary.

Proposition SM3.14 (Continuous dependence of loss processes on the contagion matrix) . Fix 

T > 0. Let őď, őď‚Ä≤ ‚ąą RK√óK

be two contagion matrices, and assume that they satisfy the uniform boundedness condition

The two McKean‚ÄďVlasov systems have the same initial distribution, the same drift functions, the same diffusion coefficients, the same control process, and the same kill intensity. Assume also that 

bk is Lipschitz in the state variable and measure variables, that őĽk is Lipschitz in the state variable, and that 

0 ‚Č§ őĽk(x, a ) ‚Č§ őõőĽ.

Let (őľkt (őď) , L kt (őď) ) 

> 0‚Č§t‚Č§T

, (őľkt (őď ‚Ä≤), L kt (őď ‚Ä≤))

> 0‚Č§t‚Č§T

denote the limiting surviving measures and loss processes under contagion matrices őď and őď‚Ä≤,respectively. If both limiting systems are well posed, then there exists a constant 

CT = C(T, K, L b, L őĽ, őõőĽ, őõőď

such that for each k = 1 , . . . , K ,

> 0‚Č§t‚Č§T

Equivalently, conditional on the common-noise path, the same estimate holds almost surely for the conditional loss processes. In particular, if the main text uses the same constant őõ to uniformly dominate the upper bound on the kill intensity and the common upper bound on the contagion matrices, then the constant may be written as C(T, őõ) . Thus the limiting loss process is Lipschitz continuous with respect to the contagion matrix in L1, and pathwise after conditioning on the common noise. Proof. Remark SM3.15 (Case without common noise) . 76 In addition, assume that the controlled martingale problem is well posed for every progressively measurable compact-valued control. The dynamic-programming and viscosity framework follows the standard infinite-dimensional control approach of Fabbri‚ÄďGozzi‚ÄďSwiech [ 41 ]. The comparison-principle argument for the present killed Wasserstein HJB is formulated after the decomposition 

őľk = őĹk + Lkőī‚ąā , with alive finite measures and cemetery masses treated as separate coordinates. It combines the Wasserstein smooth-gauge method of Cosso‚ÄďGozzi‚ÄďKharroubi‚ÄďPham‚ÄďRosestolato [ 43 ]for the alive-measure variables with the paper-specific finite-dimensional estimates for the cemetery-state killing jump. For the existence statement, admissible controls are taken in the relaxed sense. A relaxed control is a progressively measurable probability-measure-valued process 

ŌĎt ‚ąą P ([0 , ¬Įa]2),

and strict controls correspond to Dirac relaxed controls ŌĎt = őīat . Coefficients and costs under ŌĎt are obtained by averaging the corresponding strict-control coefficients over [0 , ¬Įa]2. Because the action set is compact and the coefficients are continuous in the action, this relaxed admissible class is compact under weak convergence. 

SM4.1 Comparison principle for the killed two-population HJB 

This subsection proves the comparison-principle interface for the killed two-population HJB. The proof is written on the decomposed state space 

D2 := 

> {

(őĹ1, őĹ 2, L 1, L 2) : őĹk ‚ąą M ‚Č§1,2(R), L k ‚ąą [0 , 1] , |őĹk| + Lk = 1

The Wasserstein smooth-gauge/Ishii template is used for the alive-measure coordinates őĹk, and the ceme-tery masses Lk are handled by the finite-dimensional doubled-variable penalty ő≤|L‚ąíM |2/2. The only term outside the standard alive-measure template is the mass transfer from őĹk to Lk; Lemma SM4.5 verifies that this killing jump is a bounded Lipschitz first-order perturbation compatible with the doubled-variable estimate. Throughout this subsection the control set is the compact interval [0 , ¬Įa]2. Let 

E = R ‚ą™ { ‚ąā}

be endowed with an auxiliary metric d‚ąā that agrees with the Euclidean metric on compact alive-state sets and separates the cemetery point from R. This metric is used only to identify (őĹk, L k)

with the killed probability measure őĹk + Lkőī‚ąā and to define localization estimates for unmatched alive/cemetery mass; the comparison argument itself is carried out on D2. The localization is removed by the usual coercive second-moment penalty in the Wasserstein comparison argument. Equivalently, one may work directly with a metric such as d‚ąā (x, y ) = |x ‚ąí y| on R and d‚ąā (x, ‚ąā ) = 1 + |x|. We extend the kill intensity by őĽk(‚ąā, a ) = 0 . Proof. The decomposition follows from the disjoint union E = R ‚ą™ { ‚ąā}. A signed perturbation supported on R changes only the alive finite measure őĹk, and therefore gives the identity of the alive functional derivatives. This is the standard finite-dimensional part of the doubling argument: if this quantity stayed bounded away from zero, replacing one loss vector by the other while keeping the other coordinates fixed would increase the doubled functional up to a modulus-of-continuity error smaller than the penalty gain. Proposition SM4.6 (Wasserstein smooth-gauge/Ishii interface) . After the state decomposition in Lemma SM4.4, the killed two-population HJB reduces to a comparison problem on D2. On this decomposed space, the alive-measure part satisfies the structural hypotheses of the Wasserstein smooth-gauge comparison template, while the cemetery killing jump is controlled by Lemma SM4.5. The reference template is [ 43 , Assumptions (A)‚Äď(D), Lemma 4.4, Theorems 4.5 and 5.1]. The Hamiltonian is proper, continuous in the state variables under W2 √ó W2 √ó | ¬∑ | , locally uniformly continuous in the first-order jets on bounded jet sets, and degenerate elliptic in the second-order jets. The diagonal idiosyncratic terms are nondegenerate in the alive directions because ŌÉk > 0, and the common-noise term has the usual positive-semidefinite product-measure covariance form. It is thus a reduction to the standard alive-measure smooth-gauge argument plus the finite-dimensional cemetery-mass estimate proved here. 

81 Proof. The cited comparison argument follows the viscosity doubling-variable framework of Crandall, Ishii, and Lions [ 42 ] in its Wasserstein smooth-gauge form, and assumes bounded continuous coefficients with Lipschitz dependence on the state and the measure variable, compactness of the control set, sufficient regularity of the diffusion coefficient for the smooth-gauge construction, bounded uniformly continuous terminal data, coercive localization on the unbounded second-moment state space, properness, local continuity of the Hamiltonian along admissible Wasserstein jets, and a degenerate-elliptic second-order structure. In the present model these conditions are verified after the alive‚Äďloss decomposition as follows. The Hamiltonian contains no zeroth-order dependence on the value itself, so properness follows directly. The control set is compact and the population-weighted running costs are continuous. The drift and contagion feedback are Lipschitz in the alive state, measure variables, and loss variables; Lemma SM4.3 gives the same continuity for the aggregate kill rates appearing in both the feedback drift and the loss drift. Lemma SM4.2 gives the continuity estimate for the alive-measure sink. The diffusion coefficients are constants, so the second-order part is continuous in the state and monotone in the second-order jet in the viscosity order. The idiosyncratic covariance matrices are positive definite in the alive directions under ŌÉk > 0, and the common-noise covariance matrix is positive semidefinite; hence the full second-order operator has the ellipticity structure required in the smooth-gauge/Ishii comparison proof. The only additional term relative to the template is the cemetery killing jump, and Lemma SM4.5 supplies the needed doubled-variable estimate for that term. The coercive second-moment penalty gives compactness of maximizing sequences before the localization radius is removed; the bounded uniformly continuous terminal condition preserves the terminal ordering, and the common-noise second-order term enters through the positive-semidefinite product covariance already allowed by the template.

Take a minimizing sequence of relaxed controls (ŌĎn)n‚Č•1, and let (őľ1,n , őľ 2,n ) be the corresponding measure flows. Since [0 , ¬Įa]2 is compact, the space P([0 , ¬Įa]2) is compact under weak convergence. The bounded kill intensity and the linear-growth drift give tightness of the corresponding controlled measure flows in 

C([ t, T ]; P2(E)2).

By Prokhorov‚Äôs theorem and the compactness of relaxed controls, there is a subsequence along which the relaxed control‚Äďstate laws converge weakly. By the stability of the controlled martingale problem under weak convergence in Assumption SM4.1, the limiting state process satisfies the relaxed version of (20) ; denote the limiting relaxed control by ŌĎ‚ąó. By lower semicontinuity of the running cost and continuity of the terminal cost, 

J(t, őľ 1, őľ 2; ŌĎ‚ąó) ‚Č§ lim inf  

> n‚Üí‚ąě

J(t, őľ 1, őľ 2; ŌĎn) = V (t, őľ 1, őľ 2).

Thus ŌĎ‚ąó is an optimal relaxed control. The compact relaxed-control problem admits an optimal relaxed control. Under the standard chattering condition, every relaxed control can be approximated in the weak occupation-measure topology by progressively measurable strict controls, and the stability estimate (34) transfers this approximation to the measure flow and terminal cost. The relaxed value then agrees with the closure of strict-control values. If the pointwise Hamiltonian minimizer admits a progressively measurable selector, selecting this minimizer gives a strict feedback control attaining the same value. This proves (23). We now establish the dynamic programming principle. By (33) , the controlled measure flow depends continuously on the current state. By (36) and (37) , the value function is uniformly continuous on the Wasserstein state space. The controlled martingale problem is stable under concatenation of controls. The comparison theorem proved in Theorem SM4.7 applies to the killed two-population HJB under the bounded Lipschitz killing, compact-control, Lipschitz-drift, constant-diffusion, and alive-diffusion nondegeneracy assumptions above. Hence any bounded uniformly continuous viscosity subsolution and supersolution ordered at terminal time remain ordered on [0 , T ]. Applying this result to two viscosity solutions of (24) gives uniqueness in the class of bounded uniformly continuous Wasserstein‚ÄďLions viscosity solutions. The value function V constructed by dynamic programming is therefore the unique viscosity solution of this HJB equation in that class. The viscosity characterization above does not require classical differentiability of the value function. The following first-order condition is a smooth-verification statement: assume that, on the region under consideration, a strict Hamiltonian minimizer exists and the value admits Lions derivatives that identify the marginal cemetery-jump and loss-feedback terms. The relaxed formulation gives the same Hamiltonian because the generator and the population-weighted running cost are averaged linearly over actions, so the infimum over relaxed actions is attained at an extreme point whenever a strict measurable minimizer exists. Remark SM4.13 (Differentiability of the conditional expected terminal cost) . The twice Gateaux differentiability assumption is imposed on the conditional expected terminal cost, not on individual sample-path default indicators. Individual default indicators are discontinuous as path functionals, but the regularized killing construction and conditional averaging over the killed-particle law provide the smoothed object used in the variational argument. A sufficient local regime is the following. On the control window, take bounded compact controls, assume the drift and kill intensity are twice continuously differentiable in the state and control variables with bounded derivatives on the relevant compact state region, assume the exponential kill intensity is clipped above by the bounded-killing cap used in the main model, and take a bounded twice continuously differentiable terminal penalty ő¶. If the active window stays away from saturation and from degenerate zero-intensity configurations, then the compensator of the Cox killing construction and the conditional killed-particle law are twice Gateaux differentiable with respect to bounded control perturbations by the standard dominated-differentiation argument for SDE coefficients and Cox intensities. The constants in Assumption SM4.9 can then be chosen from the corresponding first- and second-variation bounds. We use this differentiability as a verification-type regularity condition for the information-value theorem; it can degrade in small samples, near control caps, or near degenerate threshold configurations.

This subsection gives a primitive verification of the steep-killing bridge. The argument derives eventual entrance of the steep-killing fixed points into a neighborhood of the absorbing fixed point, local stability of the absorbing fixed point, and uniform convergence of the driven steep-killing maps to the absorbing-boundary map from a localized regularity package: compact localization, a uniformly nondegenerate idiosyncratic diffusion coefficient near the boundary, uniform one-dimensional killed-diffusion estimates, and uniqueness of the absorbing-boundary McKean‚ÄďVlasov fixed point in the localized class. Throughout, the common noise is retained. All estimates below may be read conditionally on the common-noise path and then integrated over the common noise. After conditioning on the common noise, the term ŌÉ0dW 0

is a given continuous forcing path and does not provide conditional smoothing. Hence the nondegeneracy assumption is placed on the idiosyncratic coefficient ŌÉk, not on ŌÉ2

Primitive regularity package 

Assumption SM4.15 (Localized primitive regularity) . The following conditions hold on [0 , T ].(P1) Compact localization. For each type k, alive states are localized in a compact interval 

Ik = [ xk, x k], with xb,k ‚ąą (xk, x k). Equivalently, the original unbounded model is stopped at a large compact interval and all estimates below are applied before the localization error is removed. (P2) Bounded Lipschitz frozen coefficients. The drift bk(t, x, z t, a ) is bounded and Lipschitz in (x, z t), uniformly in (t, a ). The controls take values in a compact set. There are constants 

0 < őĽ ‚Č§ őĽ < ‚ąě such that 

őĽ ‚Č§ ¬ĮőĽk(akt ) ‚Č§ őĽ for all k, t. 

The lower bound is satisfied by the exponential regulatory-control specification on any compact control set. (P3) Conditional nondegeneracy. The idiosyncratic volatility is uniformly positive near the boundary: 

ŌÉk ‚Č• ŌÉ > 0, k = 1 , . . . , K. 

This condition is imposed on ŌÉk, rather than on ŌÉ2 

> k

+ ŌÉ20 , because the arguments are conditional on the common noise. (P4) Uniform killed-diffusion estimates. For every compact class of frozen inputs satisfying the moment and modulus bounds used below, the one-dimensional diffusion killed at xb,k 

has transition densities and survival probabilities satisfying the standard Aronson and boundary-gradient bounds, uniformly over that class. In particular, for bounded measurable 

f , the killed semigroup P k,z s,t f (x) := Es,x [f (Xk,z t )1{Hzk >t }] satisfies 

‚ą•‚ąāxP k,z s,t f ‚ą•‚ąě ‚Č§ C(t ‚ąí s)‚ąí1/2‚ą•f ‚ą•‚ąě, 0 ‚Č§ s < t ‚Č§ T, 

with a constant C independent of z in the class. The same estimates hold for the baseline-Cox killed semigroup, since the baseline killing rate is bounded. 107 (P5) Uniform absorbing no-atom modulus. For each compact frozen-input class considered below, the absorbing default times have a uniform distributional modulus: there is a deterministic modulus ŌČabs (h) ‚Üď 0 as h ‚Üď 0 such that 

sup 

> z

sup 

> 0‚Č§t‚Č§t+h‚Č§T

P(t < ŌĄ abs ,z k ‚Č§ t + h) ‚Č§ ŌČabs (h).

This condition is required only for the absorbing default time. No uniform-in-n no-atom modulus is imposed on ŌĄ n,z k , since the steep intensity has a Lipschitz and size constant that grows exponentially in n inside the localized interval. Compactness of the steep fixed-point loss paths will instead use monotonicity, Helly selection, and the continuity of the absorbing limiting loss path. (P6) Absorbing fixed-point class. The localized absorbing-boundary McKean‚ÄďVlasov map 

ő¶abs is considered on the compact class generated by (P1)‚Äď(P5). Its fixed-point uniqueness is not imposed as a separate black-box assumption; it is verified below from the Volterra stability estimate and a short-time contraction‚Äďconcatenation argument. 

Remark SM4.16 (Why only the absorbing modulus is imposed) . The absorbing no-atom modulus in (P5) is the regularity input that controls continuity of the boundary-default map at the level of loss paths. This condition follows from one-dimensional nondegenerate killed-diffusion density estimates and bounded baseline Cox killing. It is imposed only on the absorbing default time and should not be required for ŌĄ n,z k uniformly in n: on a compact interval the bound on őĽ(n) 

> k

is of order exp (Cn ),so a direct density bound for the steep default time would degenerate with n. The proof below avoids such a false uniform modulus. Uniform convergence of the maps is obtained from the post-hit occupation and clock-mismatch estimate, while compactness of the fixed-point loss paths uses Helly selection and the eventual identification of every limit as the continuous absorbing fixed point.

Proposition SM4.24 (Localized uniqueness of the absorbing fixed point) . Lemma SM4.25 (Compactness of the steep fixed-point family) . Assume the primitive package and let z(n) be fixed points of ő¶n. Then every sequence of steep fixed points has a subsequence, still denoted by z(nj ), with the following compactness properties. The loss paths converge by Helly selection at all continuity points of nondecreasing limits Lk, ‚ąó, and the lifted laws are tight in the bounded-Lipschitz path topology after this Helly extraction. If the resulting limiting loss paths are continuous, then the Helly convergence of losses is uniform on [0 , T ], and the corresponding subsequence converges in DT . In particular, once the limit is identified as the continuous absorbing fixed point, the convergence is in the full DT metric. Proof. Under compact localization the second-moment bound is immediate. This supplies the loss compactness in the natural Helly topology. For the alive-state component, use tightness at fixed times and the SDE increment estimate away from the jump into the cemetery state. Brownian increments contribute order h1/2, bounded drift contributes order h, and the finite-variation contagion feedback is controlled along convergent subsequences by the Helly convergence of the loss paths. The only discontinuity of the lifted state is the cemetery jump, whose contribution to the bounded-Lipschitz modulus is exactly controlled by the loss increment. Hence, after passing to a Helly subsequence for the losses, the lifted laws are tight in the bounded-Lipschitz path topology. Prokhorov compactness at fixed times follows from the uniform second-moment bound, and the preceding increment estimate gives the required equicontinuity on every subsequence whose losses have a continuous limit. This proves the stated subsequential compactness, and the final assertion follows from the Helly‚ÄďP√≥lya upgrade when the limit is continuous.

z(n) ‚ąą U for all sufficiently large n.

P√≥lya‚Äôs theorem upgrades the Helly convergence of Lk, (nj ) to uniform convergence on [0 , T ]; the lifted-law convergence then upgrades to DT by the SDE stability and the cemetery-jump control in Lemma SM4.25. inside the localized class contains all sufficiently large z(n).
